CONCEPT OF SRi AS PURUSAKARA ACCORDING TO THE PURUSAKARA MMMSA OF NĀRĀYAṆA MUNI

THESIS SUBMITTED TO THE UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS FOR THE AWARD OF THE DEGREE OF DOCTOR OF PHILOSOPHY IN VAISHNAVISM

RESEARCH SCHOLAR : Mrs G. SAKUNTHALA, M A. M.Phil Department of Vaishnavism University of Madras Madras - 600 005

RESEARCH SUPERVISOR : DR M. NARASIMHACHARY Professor & Head Department of Vaishnavism University of Madras Madras - 600 005

DEPARTMENT OF VAISHNAVISM UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS MADRAS - 600 005

JULY 1992

[[P2]] UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS DEPARTMENT OF VAISNAVISM

DR M. NARASIMHACHARY, M.A. (TEL)., M.A. (SKT)., PH.D., PROFESSOR & HEAD OF THE DEPARTMENT OF VAISHNAVISM, UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS, MADRAS - 600 005.

CERTIFICATE

This is to certify that the thesis entitled THE CONCEPT OF SRI AS PURUSAKARA ACCORDING TO THE PURUSAKARA MIMAMSA OF NARAYANA MUNI submitted by MRS G. SAKUNTHALA is a record of research work done by the candidate during the period of study under me (July 1988 to July 1992) and that the thesis has not formed the basis of any Degree, Diploma, Associateship Fellowship or other similar titles. The thesis represents an independent work on the part of the candidate prepared under my general supervision.

SUPERVISOR

[[P4]] ii

These basic points became more elaborate and complicated when Her role as the “upaya” (means) and “upeya” (goal) was discussed. Thus we find that even among those who claim allegiance to Vedanta Deśika, sharp differences arose regarding Lakṣmī’s nature and causal character. The Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā and the Śrītattva Siddhāñjana are the classical instances for this post-Deśika outlook. The Śrītattva Siddhāñjana was written by Śrī Vedānta Rāmānuja with the sole aim of “demolishing” the views of Nārāyaṇa Muni regarding the nature and status of Lakṣmī as the means of liberation, the source of world-manifestation, etc. From the references found in the Śrītattva Siddhāñjana (“kəcid arvāñcaḥ”) it is probable that Vedānta Rāmānuja was a junior contemporary of Nārāyaṇa Muni, the author of the Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā. While the Śrītattvasiddhāñjana became popular among the Vaḍagalai scholars and has been printed more than once, the text of the Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā has been subjected to comparative neglect, which it does not deserve, as is made out in the present Thesis.

The Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā is available in the form of an unpublished palm-leaf manuscript deposited in the Government Oriental Manuscripts Library (GOML) under the Accession No. MT 406. It has an elaborate Commentary “Maṇidīpikā” by Śrīśaileśa in the form of an unpublished palm-leaf manuscript consisting of 215 leaves, deposited in the same Library under Acc. No. R. 3147. This work is complete but for a lacuna towards the end of the text, while the end-portion is itself in tact.

[[P5]] iii

Śrīśaileśa’s commentary reveals a rare spirit of reconciliation and rapport, a sincere urge for synthesis and a genuine desire to effect compromise between the Teṅkalai and Vaḍagalai sects. Indeed Nārāyaṇa Muni himself exhibits this spirit of reconciliation towards the end of his work. He mentions by name Piḷḷai Lokācārya and Vedānta Deśika and pronounces that men of wisdom should try to resolve all differences and apparent contradictions by identifying the ultimate import of these great Ācāryas. All right-thinking people agree that this spirit of reconciliation and synthesis is the need of the hour for resurrecting the image of the glorious Śrīvaiṣṇava tradition which has earlier suffered considerably more by its own inner factions than due to any serious threat from outside. It is with this idea that my Guru, Prof. M. Narasimhachary suggested that I take up the Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā for study. This text, along with the Maṇidīpikā has not so far formed the subject of any serious and sustained study. While the Puruṣakāramīmāṁsā had been briefly studied by scholars, the Maṇidīpikā has not been noticed even by scholars which is a desideratum.

The present study has been divided into six chapters. Chapter I deals with the date and works of Nārāyaṇa Muni, his predecessors and their contribution and other details down to the times of the author and his arch-critic Vedānta Rāmānuja. Chapter II deals with the concept of Śakti which is the basis for the concept of Śrī from Vedic times, tracing in the process, the development of the

[[P6]] iv

concept through the centuries. Special attention has been paid to the concept as found in the Pāñcarātra Āgamas and in the elaborate stotras and commentaries of later Ācāryas of the Śrīvaiṣṇava school. Chapter III deals with the main differences between Vedānta Rāmānuja and Nārāyaṇa Muni on the basis of their works. Special care has been taken to study Nārāyaṇa Muni’s views with the help of the commentary “Maṇidīpikā”. Chapter IV gives an analysis of the Text along with the Commentary. All the important views on Lakṣmī have been explicitly stated and explained. Chapter V traces the contribution of Śrīśaileśa to the Śrīvaiṣṇava thought based on his Maṇidīpikā, drawing special attention to his remarkable spirit of synthesis and compromise in trying to bring the two sects closer. Chapter VI gives the Conclusion, summarising the entire study, highlighting the contribution of Nārāyaṇa Muni to the Śrīvaiṣṇava philosophy in general and to the concept of Śrī as Puruṣakāra in particular.

It is now my pleasant duty to acknowledge gratefully the help and cooperation I received directly and indirectly from individuals and Institutions, in preparing this Thesis. I am also grateful to them for granting me the University Research Fellowship from December 1991 till date.

[[P7]] v

He suggested the present topic for research and has been helping me with enormous patience in all aspects of this work from reading the Palm-leaf manuscript, preparing the basic text, studying it analytically and presenting the results in the form of a scholarly work. It must be stated in this connection that preparing the basic text of the Puruṣakāramīmāṁsā along with the Maṇidīpikā and studying it became quite challenging because of its sheer voluminousness and the subtleties of difference between Nārāyaṇa Muni and Vedānta Rāmānuja Muni. But my task has been rendered easier and enjoyable when my Guru explained the text to me, spending countless hours in giving a shape to this Thesis and clearing my doubts and questions. But for his kind help I would not have been able to complete this task. For all this and for the personal interest he took in my academic progress, I am always beholden to him.

[[P13]]

NĀRĀYAṆA MUNI, PREDECESSORS

CHAPTER I NĀRĀYAṆA MUNI - LIFE AND WORKS HIS PREDECESSORS - THEIR WORKS AND OTHER DETAILS

The Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā (PM) of Nārāyaṇa Muni with the commentary of Maṇidīpikā (MD) of Śrīśaileśa of the Vātsya family forms the subject matter of the present study. The PM is an important text in the Śrīvaiṣṇava literature because it discusses the role of Lakṣmī as mediator between man and God. It also discusses various other aspects such as Her being the upāya or upeya of human endeavour. The text is in the form of simple and succinct Anuṣṭubh verses which are 182 in number. There is a popular view among a section of scholars that the author of this work was the immediate successor of Ādivan Śaṭhakopa, (A.D. 1398-1458) founder of the Ahobila Maṭha.1 But gathering information from the commentary Maṇidīpikā it can be understood that another Nārāyaṇa Muni, the 14th pontiff of the Maṭha could have been the author of the PM, but not the second pontiff. It is accepted beyond any doubt that the second pontiff Nārāyaṇa

[[P14]] 2

Muni I wrote several works such as Bhāva-pradīpikā, Puruṣārtha Sudhānidhi, Rahasyatrayajīvātu and Ṣaṣṭi-prabandha. The authorship of the PM, however, cannot be attributed to him.

Date of Nārāyaṇa Muni I the second pontiff :

Since Ādivan Śaṭhakopa is placed between A.D.1398-1458 his successor Nārāyaṇa Muni must have also flourished in the first half of the fifteenth century. If Ādivan Śaṭhakopa is believed to have held the pontifical chair till his end, i.e., upto the year A.D.1458, Nārāyaṇa Muni might have succeeded him during the same year or the succeeding year, i.e., in A.D. 1458 or A.D.1459. Since it is believed that the third pontiff Parāṅkuśa I ascended the throne in A.D. 1473, we may conclude that Nārāyaṇa Muni the second pontiff, must have held his pontificate till A.D. 1472 or A.D.1473. Therefore his date may tentatively be assigned to A.D. 1459-1472 or 1473.

The Date of Śrīśaileśa:

The exact date of Śrīśaileśa the author of the commentary Maṇidīpikā on the PM, is not quite certain. Given below is a passage found towards the end of the

[[P15]] 3

commentary which throws some light upon the parentage and lineage of Śrīśaileśa.

श्रीवत्स्यराजगोपालतनयं तत्पदाश्रयम्। अम्माकार्या कृपापात्रं श्रीनिवासगुरुं भजे।।

At the beginning of the commentary also we find the following statements regarding his lineage:

श्रीवत्स्यराजगोपालगुरुं मन्तातमाश्रये। यत्सेवया मया लब्धं श्रीशान्तत्त्वावबोधनम्।।

From these passages we understand that Śrīśaileśa was the son of Vātsya Rājagopāla. He was a disciple of Nārāyaṇa Muni (14th pontiff) who himself was a disciple of Śrī Vīrarāghava the thirteenth Jīyar. Śrīśaileśa’s brother was one Śrīnivāsa who, according to the Maṇidīpikā-verse was “Ammaḷārya - kṛpāpātram”, recipient of the grace of “Ammaḷārya”. The question that has to be resolved now is as to who this Ammaḷārya was. We know that all the Ācāryas born in the family of Vātsya Varada are known to have held the title “Ammaḷārya”. The fifth descendent of Vātsya Varada, viz., Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ was popularly known by the name “Ammaḷārya”, although his real name was Varadācārya. If from the statement of Śrīśaileśa we can

[[P16]] 4

deduce that his brother Śrīnivāsa was graced by Ammaḷārya we may have to conclude that he was a contemporary of Ammaḷārya who lived between A.D. 1325 and 1400.2 But this would clash with the date of Nārāyaṇa Muni the 14th Jīyar whose date is given as A.D. 1676.3

This discrepancy can be got over when we take this Ammaḷārya as yet another descendant of the Vātsya family but not as Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ. Further it is quite evident from the statements made by Śrīśaileśa in his Maṇidīpikā that he was the disciple of Nārāyaṇa Muni who was the disciple of Vīrarāghava:

श्रीवीरराघवमुनेर्विरस्यैकजीवम्। समाश्रयेमहि श्रीमन्नारायणमुनीश्वरम्।।4

The following passage found towards the end of the Maṇidīpikā would point to the exact identity of the author of the PM.

[[P17]]

नाशायणमुनिनैश्यनेन संप्रदायवैलक्षण्यं प्रदर्शितम्। सर्वतन्त्रस्वतन्त्र - श्रीमच्छठकोप - योगीन्द्र दिव्यपरम्परामुप्राविधानं पूर्वषां श्रीमन्नारायणयोगीन्द्राणां दास्यस्युकेन नारायणपदेन भगवन्मननादि शीलनासूचकेन मुनिनेति पदेन च तथाविध दिव्यपरम्परा लब्ध - विशुद्ध सम्प्रदाय शानिशुक्तिं भवति।

In this passage Śrīśaileśa states that the author of PM was preceded by other pontiffs who also bore the name ‘Nārāyaṇa’. The words “pūrveṣāṁ śrīmannārāyaṇayogīndrāṇām” (in plural) deserves careful consideration. These words definitely point to the fact that the author of the PM could not have been the second pontiff who immediately succeeded Ādivan Śaṭhakopa the founder of the Ahobila Maṭha. In fact, between him and his predecessor Ādivan Śaṭhakopa, there was no other Ācārya even according to the canonical list of teachers (Guruparamparā) handed down to us.

Another important fact that corroborates this finding is that Śrīśaileśa very often quotes from the Śrītattvasiddhāñjana (ŚTS) of Vedānta Rāmānuja Muni (Śākṣāt svāmī).

[[P18]] Vedānta Rāmānuja Muni wrote his ŚTS with the sole view of demolishing the findings of the PM.5 He was the disciple of Kalyāṇāvaha Raṅganātha Mahādeśika, was born in Koppal Agrahāram, a village near Thirunārāyaṇapuram in the Karnataka State.6 It is said that he resembled Bhāṣyakāra (Śrī Rāmānuja) himself for which reason he was also known as “Śākṣāt Svāmī”. Vedānta Rāmānuja Muni has been reverentially quoted in the Nyāyavidyāpariṣkṛti of unknown authorship.7 His other works include Divyasūri-prabhāva-Dīpikā, commentaries in Maṇipravāḷa called8

श्रीमद्वेदान्तरामानुजमुनेः कृतिषु पुरुषकारमीमांसादि-नानाग्रन्थनिरासकं श्रीतत्त्वसिद्धान्जनं संपूर्णम्।।

[[P19]] 7

“Irupatti Nālayirappaḍi” (24000 Granthas) on Thirukkurugai-ppirān Piḷḷān’s Ārāyirappaḍi (6000 Granthas) and “Irupatti-nālayirappaḍi Śabdārtha” on Thiruvāymoḻi.9 He also wrote Pūrvasārasvādinī on the first twelve Adhikaraṇas of the Rahasyatrayasāra of Vedānta Deśika in addition to the Bṛhadguruparamparāsārasvādinī Vyākhyāna and the Nyāyavidyādarpana. His disciple was Gopāla Deśika, who was the founder of the Munitraya Sampradāya. His year of birth is mentioned as A.D. 1701.10 By this we may approximately fix the date of his guru Vedānta Rāmānuja Muni as the end of the 17th cent. When we compare Nārāyaṇa Muni the 14th Jīyar’s date (A.D.1676) with this we will be convinced that they could have been contemporaries. It is quite possible that Vedānta Rāmānuja differed from his contemporary Nārāyaṇa Muni on certain aspects of Lakṣmī’s role, and hence he could have written the STS criticising the PM. Śrīśaileśa tries to defend the views of PM against the criticism of STS.

As understood from the concluding part of the Maṇidīpikā the author of PM, Nārāyaṇa Muni represents

[[P20]] 8

the great tradition of the pontiffs of the Ahobila Maṭha leaded by Ādivan Śaṭhakopa who himself was the disciple of Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ the fifth descendant of Vātsya Varadācārya (VV). VV was also known as Āmbāḷ (same as Ammāḷ in Tamil) which ultimately means Naḍādūr Ammāḷ by which name he was popularly known.11

In this context it will not be out of place to trace the genealogy or Guruparamparā of the Ahobila Maṭha starting with Varada Viṣṇu Miśra who, for all practical purposes, appears to have inspired this tradition.

Varada Viṣṇu Miśra (A.D. 10th cent-11th cent) also known as Naḍādūr Āḻvān, was the grandfather of Naḍādūr Ammāḷ alias Vātsya Varadācārya. He was a native of Kāñcī and a great logician. Vedānta Deśika quotes him in his work Nyāya siddhāñjana. Of the 74 siṁhāsanasthāpatis who were authorised by Śrī Rāmānuja to carry on the Śrīvaiṣṇava religious-preaching, four

हस्तिगिरिनाथकृपालब्ध - अम्बापद … etc.

[[P21]] 9

were selected specially for expounding the Śrībhāṣya.12

The Guruparamparā refers to Varada Viṣṇu thus:

yasmin padam yativarasya mukhāt prapetum niṣkrāmadeva nicadhe nigamāntabhāgyam | tasyaiva taṁ bhagavatap priyabhāgineyam vande gurum varadavigņupadābhidhānam.13

पीठाधीशशंख कौक्षिस्यनुसूचिगणानायुक्त सप्तत्यभिरभ्येत्यान् तत्राप्यध्यक्षशंख कौक्षिते चतुश्ह तथा भाष्यसिंह्यासनेश। तत्र ज्ञानामृते ते तदुभयनिगमान्ता यभाष्यवेक्ष्या श्रीमन् रामानुजार्यो यतिनृपतिमुह्यदेशिकेन्द्रप्रकारः।। See also Krishnaswamy Iyengar’s edn. Prapannāmṛtam (Part I) with ‘Prapannāmṛta-darpaṇa’ (1983) p.591. तेजो प्रियतमा, शिष्याः बहवः संति वेणुजाः। नित्यंकेर्य निरताः यतीन्द्रस्य जगद्गुरोः।। श्रीमन् वरदविष्णुवार्यः करेष्ठाः भद्रवर्गः। श्रीभाष्यस्य यतीन्द्रेण व्याख्याने च सहायकाः।। See also V.Rangachari, “The successors of Rāmanuja and the growth of sectarianism among the Śrīvaiṣṇavas” JBRAS XXIV (1968-70), pp. 102-135. See also Ālkoṇḍavilli Govindāchārya, The Life of Rāmānujācārya, p.218.

[[P22]] 10

Varada Viṣṇu’s son Devarājaperumāḷ, one of the imhasanādhipatis, is referred to by Śrīśaileśa to be as great as his father.14 But his son Vātsya Varada ccording to Śrīśaileśa was an outstanding teacher who ad the title ‘Ambā’ conferred upon him by Lord Varadarāja imself.15

Vātsya Varadācārya was the disciple of Engaḷ Āḻvān r Viṣṇucitta (commentator on the Viṣṇupurāṇa). Ghaṭikā-atam Ammāḷ the descendant of Vātsya Varada, refers to im as Śrī Rāmānuja himself, born a second time so as to reserve the Śrībhāṣya.16

Vātsya Varada’s disciple was Kiḍāmbi Appuḷḷār or treya Rāmānuja who was also the guru of his nephew rī Vedānta Deśika. Deśika’s son was Kumāra Varadācārya r Nainārācārya who also studied under his father. Deśika ad two more disciples – Brahmatantra Svatantra Svāmī nd Prativādibhayankaram Appan.

[[P23]] Given below is the genealogical table starting with Śrī Rāmānuja and ending with Ātreya Rāmānuja as given by Śrī V. Rangacari in his article “The Successors of Rāmānuja and the growth of Sectarianism among Śrīvaiṣṇavas” (1138-1310).17

ŚRĪ RĀMĀNUJA

Kiḍāmbi Āccān (Bhāṣya Siṁhāsanādhipati) Piḷḷān (Ubhaya Siṁhasanādhipati) Naḍādūr Āḻvān or Varada Viṣṇu Miśra (Bhāṣya siṁhāsanādhipati) Mudali Āṇḍān (Bhāṣya siṁhasanādhipati)

[[P24]] 12

In this illustrious family was born Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ, as the son of Ghaṭikāśatam Sudarśana, the celebrated author of Śrutaprakāśikā on Rāmānuja’s Śrībhāṣya.

Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ studied under Brahmatantra Svāmin and Nainārācārya.18 Thus Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ19 becomes

सत्कृता स भट्टि शिष्य गाणान् अन्वयलक्ष्मेरखिलैस्स्वचिन्हैः श्रीभाष्यसिंहासनमात्मनीनम् यस्मै च दत्तं यतिशेखरैण।। तस्य वेदान्तकृद्भिः पौत्रोऽभूद्गुरोः। श्रुतप्रकाशिका ग्रन्थं यश्चित्थमपः।। तस्य पञ्चमः प्रपञ्चविदितं वैदुष्यः काञ्चीपुर - वास्तव्यः श्रीघटिकाशतं सुदर्शनाचार्यसूनुः श्रीवेदान्ताचार्ययोः दर्शनस्थापनाचार्ययोः प्रसादभूमिर्वद चार्यों नाम कविः। तद्विरचितं नाटकमाकम् … See the colophon at the end of the VI act. इति श्रीवत्सम कुलतिलक श्रीघटिकाशतसुदर्शनाचार्य-तनुभवस्य श्रीमदम्माकाचार्यपरनामधेयस्य श्रीमद्वरदाचार्य कृतिषु वेदान्तविलासापरनामधेय यतिराजविजये नाटके षष्ठो अङ्कः

[[P25]] the fifth descendant of Naḍādūr Ammāḷ. His works include Rukmiṇīpariṇaya Campu, Lakṣmīśataka, and the less known allegorical play Yatirāja Vijaya.20 He was popularly known as Ammāḷ or Ammaḷārya or Ammāḷācārya. He was known originally as Varadācārya and hence the name Vātsya Varada also applies to him. So he should not be confused with his Kiṭastha, the more popular Vātsya Varadācārya (Naḍādūr Ammāḷ). This is evident from his own statement in the Vasantatilaka Bhāṇa.21

E.V.Vīrarāghavācārya mentions that Appaya Dīkṣita in his Kuvalayānanda (A.D. 1520-1591) quotes three verses without mentioning their source, which can be traced to Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ’s Vasantatilakabhāṇa and Yatirāja Vijaya.22

काञ्चीपुरे कविवररुद्राचार्यनाम्ना शुण्ठः सुदर्शनकवि घटिकाशतस्थ। वेदान्तकं विविधार्थं विचारधीरो वात्स्यो वसन्ततिलकं वभण भणम्।। And also the colophon: काञ्चीपुरमभिवसन् कमनीय-भूमा कामीड्य हार्द वरदः … etc.

[[P26]] He further states that the Telugu Poet Gaṇapavarapu Veṅkaṭa Kavi (Circa A.D. 1650)23 had translated Vasantatilaka Bhāṇa into Telugu and that this text was also quoted anonymously by Somakavi (Circa 1750 A.D) in his commentary on the Telugu Prabandha Vasucaritamu of Rāmarājabhūṣaṇa.24 Scholars are however divided regarding his date. Vīrarāghavācārya assigns him to the later half of 14th cent and the first quarter of the 15th cent. Taking the view that Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ was a senior contemporary of Prativādibhayankaram Annan, Krishnaswamy Iyengar opines that he must have lived between A.D. 1350 and 1425.

Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ’s disciple was Ādivan Śaṭhakopa muni who was the founder and the first Jīyar of the Ahobila Maṭha.

In the light of this, a brief account of the contribution of the pontiffs who ascended the pīṭha of Ahobila Maṭha may not be out of place here.

Ādivan Śaṭhakopa flourished between A.D.1379 and

[[P27]] 15

1458.25 Vasantika Paripaya, a play in Sanskrit by the seventh pontiff of Ahobila Maṭha (A.D.1513-1522) refers to Ādivan Śaṭhakopa as one who was honoured by King Mukundadevarāya and by the then Karnataka King Prauḍha-devarāya II by offering him the Karṇāṭaka throne.26 According to Sannidhi Guruparamparā, Ādivan Śaṭhakopa became the disciple of Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ around A.D. 1391 and founded the Ahobila Maṭha in A.D.1398.

Ādivan Śaṭhakopa was the most revered scholar of his times. Many famous men resorted to his feet for spiritual guidance. Maṇavāḷa Mahāmuni (A.D.1370-1443) who is said to have been an incarnation of Śrī Rāmānuja resorted to his feet and received Praṇamantropadeśa and sannyāsa from him. Maṇappākkam Thoḷappar, also known as ‘Vaidikasārvabhauma’ who wrote treatises on Dharmaśāstra such as Smṛtiratnākara, Gṛhyaratnākara and27

पूर्वं यस्तु मुकुन्ददेवविभुना वैकण्ठो धृतान्द्रलिकः प्राप्तः श्रीपुरुषोत्तमं रचितवान् भक्त प्रतिष्ठानम्। लब्ध्वा दन्तिपुरद्ररत्नचिबिकाध्यक्षप्रदीचामर-व्रन्द्रादीनि वचो पुनश्च विजयी कर्णाटसिंहासनम्।।

[[P28]] 16

Kaṇṭhābhūṣaṇa was also a student of Ādivan Śaṭhakopa.28 His other students were Yatirāja Jīyar, Śrīman-nārāyaṇa Jīyar and Ācci Thiruveṅkaṭācāriar.29

The famous saint-composer in Telugu, “Padakavitāpitāmaha” Tāḷḷapākkam Annamācāryu (A.D.1408-1503) is said to have been a disciple of Ādivan Śaṭhakopa. He30

केळाबाय् कृपापात्रं श्रीशमादिगुणाब्धिम्। श्रीशठारिस्थितिशाणं देषिकेन्द्रमहं भजे।। प्रपद्ये निरवद्यानां शिवको गुणसम्पदाम्। कारणं भवभीतानां शठकोयमुनीश्वरम्।।

[[P29]] 17

also composed a hymn on his guru Ādivan Śaṭhakopa.31 Varada Viṣṇvācārya son of Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ learnt at the feet of Ādivan Śeṭhakopa, texts like the Śrībhāṣya, Gītābhāṣya and Bhagavad…

चूडिंदिरु सुलभुंडु हरि; तोडुनीड यगु दोरुमुनि चित्तडुं कैवल्यमुनकु गनकापु दापंबु; प्रापे श्रुतलकु हु दिपदमे; पावनमोकरूपमै विरजकु; नावै युन्नाडिकिडे थितडुं; कापाडग लोकमुलकु सुज्ञान दीपमै जगतिनि तेजमै पापालडपगा भवयोधुलकु तेपै युन्नाडिकिडे चीतडुं।। करुणानिधिकि रंगपतिकि गाचीवरुनकु। वेंकटगिरिपतिकि निरतिनहोबलुनकुकेसरिकि तत्परुंडुगु शठकोपमुनिफितडुं।।

‘Behold! this is our revered Sathakopa Muni, who is easily accessible to all and for whom Lord Hari himself is the guide and refuge. He is our golden path for final beatifice, the last word in scriptures, the soul pure and free from sin, and the boat with which to cross the river Virajā and reach Vaikuntha. He is the divine lamp of Wisdom, illuminating all the worlds, the redeemer of sins and the raft which enables us to cross this ocean called saṁsāra. He is an ardent devotee of the presiding deities of Śrīraṅgam, Veṅkaṭagiri and Ahobila and a prince among ascetics’.

[[P30]] 18

viṣayam.32

Allasāni Peddana (A.D.1509-1530) the celebrated Telugu poet who flourished in the court of King Śrī-kṛṣṇadevarāya of Vijayanagar is said to have had his literary training under one Śaṭhakopa Yati.33 He also refers to him in his works like the Harikathāsāra and Manucaritraṃ. Of these, the first text is not extant now, and only a few verses from it are quoted in Ānandaraṅgarāṭ Chandas. He also mentions in his34

The verse wherein Peddana refers to this saint is as follows:

“śaṭhakopayatiki śaṭhatru- kuṭhāropamunatikini Gurumatāhṛtkar- mathaniratikī Caturāgamī- pathanayatāniyatiki najapasambhṛtikin”.

[[P31]] 19

Manucaritramu that he obtained a village Kokaṭa (in Cuddapah District, Āndhra Pradeś) as gift from the Emperor Kṛṣṇadevarāya. Critics state that he renamed it as Śaṭhakopapura in honour of his guru and gave it away to Vaiṣṇavas.35 It is also asserted that Peddana learnt the art of poesy from this Śaṭhakopayati. Some scholars tried to identify this Śaṭhakopayati with Ādivan Śaṭhakopa, the founder of Ahobila Maṭha.36

However, since Ādivan Śaṭhakopa is said to have flourished between A.D. 1379 and 1458, and also since the period of Peddana is accepted by many scholars as A.D. 1480-1533, it appears that the Śaṭhakopa he refers to could not have been the founder of Ahobila Maṭha. He is, in all probability, the seventh pontiff of the Maṭha, whose pontificate is fixed approximately between A.D.1513 and 1522.37

“kolutun madguru vidyā- nilayun, karuṇākaṭākṣanibiḍajyotsnā- dalitāśritajannduritā- cchalagāḍhadvāntasaṁtati Śaṭhakopayatin”

[[P32]] 20

The saint who succeeded Ādivan Śaṭhakopa was Śrīvatsāṅka Śrīmannārāyaṇa Muni,38 referred in the Tamil Catalogue of Manuscripts (GOML) as ‘Śrībhāṣya Śrīmannārāyaṇa Yatīśan’.39

The periods of pontificate of the Jīyars from Ādivan Śaṭhakopa upto Nārāyaṇa Muni (the 14th Jīyar), the preceptor of Śrīśaileśa, are given below for a clear picture of the guruparamparā of the Maṭha.40

  1. Ādivan Śaṭhakopa - A.D.1378-1458
  2. Śrīmannārāyaṇa Muni (Nārāyaṇa I). - A.D.1458-1473
  3. Śrīvan Parāṅkuśa I Yatīndra Mahādeśika. - A.D. 1473-1485
  4. Śrīnivāsa Yatīndra Mahādeśika (Śrīnivāsa I). - A.D.1485-1493.
  5. Van Śaṭhakopa II - A.D.1493-1498.
  6. Ṣaṣṭha Parāṅkuśa II - A.D.1499-1513.

[[P33]] 21

  1. Śaṭhakopa III - A.D. 1513-1522
  2. Parāṅkuśa III - A.D. 1522-1538
  3. Nārāyaṇa II - A.D. 1538-1542
  4. Śaṭhakopa IV - A.D. 1542-1557
  5. Śrīnivāsa II - A.D. 1558-1596
  6. Nārāyaṇa III - A.D. 1598-1632

Vīrarāghava I, also known as Vīrarāghava Yatīndra Mahādeśika was the thirteenth Jīyar who became the pontiff in A.D.1632.

He adorned the pontifical chair till A.D. 1676. Śrīmannārāyaṇa Yatīndra Mahādeśika became the next (14th) pontiff. He was born in Puḷḷapuṭānguḍi village near Tiruvellore. He wrote Ācāryaguṇādarśa, Guruparamparāprabhāva, Īśāvāsya Bhāṣya, Muṇḍukya Bhāṣya and Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā (PM).41 However the available catalogues of books and manuscripts do not have any information on the exact authorship of these works by Nārāyaṇa Muni excepting the last work, Puruṣakāra Mīmāṁsā, which is now being studied.

[[P34]]

PURUṢAKĀRA

CHAPTER II THE CONCEPT OF PURUṢAKĀRA - ORIGIN AND DEVELOPMENT

The Śrīvaiṣṇava religion and philosophy, like all other systems of thought believes in the supremacy of one Being associated with His consort(s). As a matter of fact, conceiving the Supreme Being with spouse and offspring is anthropomorphic. But the idea is to be taken in a symbolic way in order to assess its philosophical value. That Lord Viṣṇu associated with Śrī or Lakṣmī is accepted as Supreme Brahman in the Śrīvaiṣṇava theology is accepted on all hands. But even among the followers of Śrī Rāmānuja, especially among the Teṅgalai and Vaḍagalai sects which surfaced in the 13th cent. or so, there are some subtle doctrinal differences regarding the exact status of Śrī. It is interesting to note that even among the followers of Vedānta Deśika, sharp differences on certain technical terminology and interpretations of Deśika’s words and expressions became prominent as evidenced by the Śrītattvasiddhāñjana of Vedānta Rāmāmujamuni and the Maṇidīpikā of Śrīśaileśa. These sects differ on issues such as the aṇutva or vibhutva of Śrī; Her being the direct or indirect means of liberation, Her exact relationship

[[P35]] 23

with the Lord as an attribute (viśeṣaṇa) or a pointer (upalakṣaṇa), Her being the sole cause or a partial cause of the cosmic functions such as creation and sustenance, Her being or not being capable of showing strictness etc (nigraha). But before going into the particulars of these differences it will be worthwhile to trace the concept of śakti (power) of the Supreme Being, which is conceived as the female deity. It is this concept that forms the basis of all the later developments among the different sections of religious schools.

We have to go back to the Ṛgveda for the concept of Śakti. In the Ṛgveda reference is made to ‘śakti’,42 ‘māyā’ and ‘śacī’ (in plural) to denote Divine Power. The Vedic seers were anxious to unite all minor gods into one Supreme Being called Prajāpati or Viśvakarman. Alongside this, there was also an effort to fuse all female deities such as Dhiṣaṇā and Sarasvatī, into one supreme goddess called ‘Vāk’.43 The Āmbhṛṇī Sūkta is

[[P36]] 24

the epitome of such a concept, because here Vāk declares Herself as being the source of the power of all gods, the prosperity and plenty of the land, and the intellect of the wise.44 The motherly aspect of this Śakti can be clearly seen in the expressions ambitamā and mātṛtamā (‘benign like a mother’) used to qualify the rivers Sarasvatī and Sindhu respectively.45

This concept of Śakti becomes more pronounced in Upaniṣads like the Bṛhadāraṇyaka and Śvetāśvatara. The Bṛhadāraṇyaka expressly makes Śakti the Lord’s consort using the familiar terms of ‘husband’ and ‘wife’.46 The Śvetāśvatara (VI.8) expresses the idea that the Supreme Being possesses Śakti which is supreme and manifold at the same time.47

The culmination of this concept of Śakti may be found in the ŚrīSūkta of the Ṛgveda Khila. The Taittirīya

[[P37]] 25

Āraṇyaka speaks of the Supreme as having two consorts, Hrī and Lakṣmī.48 In the Taittirīya Saṁhitā it is said that the Lord himself owes this glory to His consort called Śraddhā.49

The ŚrīSūkta is indeed the most important and the earliest piece of literature on the glory of Lakṣmī which all other ācāryas of the Śrīvaiṣṇava school follow with implicit faith and reverence for formulating their own ideas.

In this, Lakṣmī is presented as the Goddess of beauty, plenty and prosperity and also as the ruler and the Mother of the Universe.50 It is interesting to note that the expression ’tāṁ padmanemīṁ śaraṇamahaṁ prapadye’51 forms the nucleus for the Doctrine of Surrender which is characteristic of the Śrīvaiṣṇava school. The concept of Godhood can be understood and appreciated in full only when the ‘motherly’ aspect of God is recognised.

[[P38]] 26

The concept of Śrī may be said to have attained its zenith in the Pāñcarātrāgamas belonging to the Śrīvaiṣṇava religion. There Śakti is identified with Lakṣmī and Śaktimān identified with Viṣṇu. The Jayākhya Saṁhitā speaks of four powers of (śaktis) of the Lord Viṣṇu namely, Lakṣmī, Kīrti, Jayā and Māyā52 which are always associated with Him. Of these four again, Lakṣmī is the most intimately associated power of the Lord as rays to the sun and waves to the ocean.53 The Ahirbudhnya Saṁhitā (Ah.S) speaks of the identity of Śakti and Śaktimān54 and also speaks of them as two different entities.55 Lakṣmī is referred to here as Universal Mother presiding over the Nitya and Līlā Vibhūtis.

[[P39]] 27

This Saṁhitā is important for another Concept namely Her role as meditator between man and God, technically called ‘Puruṣakāra’. She is said to dispel the sins of the devotee and usher in alround prosperity which forms the nucleus of this concept. The doctrine of Prapatti to Lakṣmī can also be traced to this work.56 All other goddesses like Umā and Sarasvatī are but different modes of Lakṣmī. She is called “taddharmadharmiṇī”57 of Lord Viṣṇu showing her inseparable relation with Him and indicating the function as His consort as the coperformer of His duties. Lakṣmī is known for grace and redemption. This is clearly brought out in the various interpretations of the term Śrī given for the first time in this Saṁhitā. Later ācāryas explained these etymologies of Śrī to advantage, highlighting her role as Puruṣakāra between man and God, although the term Puruṣakāra is not used in this text. The interpretations of the term Śrī given in this text as the destroyer of all sin, the benefactor of the Universe,

[[P40]] 28

the solace of the devotee and the denizen of the Highest Heaven are worth quoting in full:

“śṛṇāti nikhilān doṣān śṛṇāti ca guṇair jagat śrīyate cākhilair nityam śrayate ca paraṁ padam” (LI.61b-62a).

“śrayantīṁ śrīyamāṇāṁ ca śṛṇatīṁ śṛṇvatīmapi” (XXI.8).

It may be pointed out in this connection that although Lakṣmī and Viṣṇu are very frequently referred to as being identical with each other by virtue of their being the Śakti and Śaktimān, they are actually regarded as different. Even at the time of Pralaya, they do not merge completely in each other but became a’ single principle “as it were”.58 It is declared that Lakṣmī alone acts but whatever she does, is the mere expression of the Lord’s wish.59

[[P41]] The Lakṣmī Tantra also speaks of Śrī as the inseparable consort of Lord Viṣṇu, the mistress of the Universe and the mother of the Creation. She is the ‘ahantā’ and the Lord is the ‘aham’. She is the Śakti and He is the Śaktimān but still, as observed in the Ahirbudhnya Saṁhitā, she appears to be different from the Lord. She is associated with the Lord as the beams are to the Moon. Various derivations of the term “Śrī” are also found here, which are slightly different from those given in the Ahirbudhnya Saṁhitā.

श्रयन्ती श्रयणीयास्मि शृणामि दुरितं सताम्। शृणोमि करुणं वाचं शृणामि च गुणैर्जगत्।। [IV. 51b - 52a]

It is also declared that Śrī is always associated with the Lord, whatever avatāra He may take from time to time.60 It may also be noted in this connection that the interpretations of the term Śrī given above, especially the expressions “śṛṇomi karuṇaṁ vācaṁ” and “śrayayipyasmi” are extremely favourable to formulate the concept of Puruṣakāra. That Lakṣmī is capable of being resorted to (śrayaṇīyā) and that she listens to

[[P42]] 30

the piteous appeals (karuṇaṁ vācam) of suffering humanity form the nucleus for Her role as the Mediator between God and man.

The Viṣṇupurāṇa is another important source of information regarding the glory and status of Lakṣmī. According to it the entire universe is a visible manifestation of the Śakti of the Lord.61 Śrī, who is called Śakti here, is glorified as ‘jaganmātā’ and ‘jagaddhātrī’.62 In about 34 verses, she is glorified as the repository of all auspicious qualities, the most prominent of which is mercy. It is because of this particular quality that Lakṣmī confers universal prosperity. She is not only viewed as the Goddess of riches in this work, but also the very embodiment of all branches of knowledge including ‘ātmavidyā’ leading to emancipation (’vimukti-phaladāyinī’).63 She is,always associated with Viṣṇu and like the Lord she

[[P43]] 31

is also ‘found everywhere’ (sarvagatā)

’nityaivaiṣā jaganmātā viṣṇośśrīranapāyinī yathā sarvagato viṣṇustathaiveyaṁ dvijottama’ (I.8.17)

The Lord is the content and Śrī is the speech. She is the scripture on Nyāya whereas He is the very teaching of the scripture.

‘artho viṣṇuh iyaṁ vāṇī, nītireṣā nayo hariḥ’ (I.8.18a).

If the Lord is Śaṅkara, Śrī becomes His consort Gaurī. If He is the Sun, she becomes His radiance:

In the ninth Adhyāya of the first aṁśa of this Purāṇa, we find the stotra on Lakṣmī made by Indra, as the Goddess emerged in all Her beauty and splendour from the milky ocean. In this beautiful piece of literature which may be described as the very first stotra, on Lakṣmī, she is called ’the mother of all the worlds’ (‘sarvalokānāṁ jananī’). The entire world becomes lost, if is given up by Lakṣmī. Her compassionate glances alone can make a man happy physically and materially. The entire world consisting of cara (mobile) and acara (immobile of stationary is pervaded by both Viṣṇu and Lakṣmī. She ……….

[[P44]] 32

is prayed not to desert the house and other kinds of possessions, the body and family-circle like wife, children and friends.64 It is again in this Purāṇa that we find the popular statement that Lakṣmī takes incarnations and appears along with the Lord, in accordance with His own manifestations.

राघवत्वेऽभवत् सीता रुक्मिणी कृष्णजन्मनि। अन्येषु चावतारेषु विष्णोरेषाऽनपायिनी।। देवत्वे देवदेहेयं मनुष्यत्वे च मानुषी। विष्णोर्देहानुरूपां वै करोत्येषाऽऽत्मनस्तनुम्।। (I.9. 144-145)

The Mārkaṇḍeya Purāṇa is unique in calling Sarasvatī the Śakti of Lord Viṣṇu. She is the universal mother and supporter (jagaddhātrī). It is stated in clear terms that when propitiated, she grants liberation to men. This power of Viṣṇu which is also called māyā is said to constitute the cause of both bondage and liberation.65

[[P45]] 33.

The Bhāgavatapurāṇa which is perhaps the last and the most important of the Purāṇas speaks of Lord Kṛṣṇa, as Supreme not as a mere manifestation of Viṣṇu but as the Supreme Bhagavān Himself. His Śakti which is manifold as vidyā and avidyā is responsible for His cosmic activities. The special designation of this Śakti is Lakṣmī also called Ramā.66

All these ideas found in the earlier literature on Śrī find a succinct doctrinal representation in the beautiful hymn Catuślokī composed by the great Śrī Vaiṣṇava philosopher Yāmunācārya. This forms the basis of all later expressions of the concept of Lakṣmī in the Śrīvaiṣṇava religion. The four verses comprising his Catuḥślokī which is also popularly known as Kāntastotra encompasses all ideas on Lakṣmī sometimes expressed and sometimes implied in the whole gamut of Śrīvaiṣṇava literature. It may be noted that some of the statements found in the Pāñcarātrāgamas and the Viṣṇu and importance as the cause of creation sustenance, dissolution, bondage and liberation of the universe. That she is as pervasive as Lord Viṣṇu Himself and that Her grace is indispensib[le]

[[P46]] 34

for all beings divine or human for any kind of welfare material or spiritual either here or in the hereafter are beautifully expressed by Yāmuna, in this Stotra. Looked against this background the Catuśślokī of Yāmuna forms the most quintessential and canonical description of Śrī,both from the viewpoint of poetry and philosophy. It may also be noted that later ācāryas like Piḷḷai Lokācārya and Vedānta Deśika base themselves on this hymn of Yāmuna to explain their subtle views on the nature, status and capabilities of Lakṣmī. It goes without saying that the stotras on Lakṣmī composed by later ācāryas like Kūreśvara, Parāśara and Vedānta Deśika are predominently inspired by this beautiful poem of Yāmuna.

The texts of Yāmuna, Rāmānuja, Kūreśa and Parāśara are important authorities which the later ācāryas like Piḷḷai Lokācārya and Vedānta Deśika dare not to criticise or condemn. Genius lies in expressing one’s view in accordance with the sanctions given by these earlier writers. Sometimes even a single word becomes subjected to a variety of interpretations bringing out very minute shades of differences. This has naturally resulted in a very vast literature on this subject. What originally

[[P47]] 35

started as a simple concept of Śakti thus became a personalised, concrete, concept of Śrī as the consort of the Lord and the Mother of all the living beings, dividing the followers of Rāmānuja into two groups in the later period as those who believe in her coeval status with Viṣṇu and those who believed in her essential character as jīva,of course with some special prerogatives. But for a general reader who goes through the Catuśślokī of Yāmuna, all these subtle ideas and differences, doctrinal and philosophical,do not at all make their appearance. For him it is a beautiful hymn inspired and inspiring at the same time. What strikes the reader most is that the author is an ardent devotee of the Goddess who feels incompetent to gauge her glory and thus humbly seeks her grace through whole-hearted surrender at her feet.

Yāmuna presents Śrī here as the mistress of the universe, the repository of all glorious qualities such as mercy (dayā), the only source of universal prosperity and as the inseparable devoted consort of the Supreme Lord Viṣṇu.

Of these four verses, the second and third are more important from the view point of Lakṣmī’s

[[P48]] 36

nature and status.67 In the second verse, Yāmuna, after stating that Lakṣmī’s glory cannot be assessed even by Her own consort Viṣṇu, observes that her quality called compassion makes him bold enough to undertake the composition of this praise. He specifically uses the terms “dāsa” and “prapanna” with reference to his ownself. Eternal servitude to the Goddess is what he asks for, and this is acquired by virtue of the prapatti he does to her. This ultimately makes him fearless (nirbhaya). Therefore it may be noted that in this verse, Yāmuna lays stress on the redemptive attitude of Śrī as the Universal Mother towards those who submit themselves to her in absolute surrender.

In the third verse, the emphasis is laid on Lakṣmī’s protective aspect.68 Yāmuna, soaring to poetic heights

[[P49]] 37

states that all creation flourishes and prospers only because of her benevolent grace (Karuṇā). There is decay and destruction when the influence of her benevolence is absent, as for example, during pralaya. Of special significance is the statement that the material and spiritual status of humanity - saṁsṛti (while in bondage) akṣara (for attaining kaivalya) and vaiṣṇavādhvā (for attaining Viṣṇu) are totally dependent upon Her grace. The categorical declaration -

श्रेयो न ह्यरविन्दलोचनमनः कान्ता प्रसादादृते। संसृत्यक्षर वैष्णवा ध्वसु नृणां संभाव्यते कर्हिचित्।।

received much attention of the later Śrīvaiṣṇava ācāryas regarding Lakṣmī’s upāyatva in the scheme of liberation.

In the fourth verse Yāmuna says that Lakṣmī (though functionally distinguishable from Viṣṇu) is (philosophically) part and parcel of Him. Right from the transcendental aspect of the Lord, all His emanations and manifestations are intrinsically and exquisitely associated with corresponding manifestations of Śrī. The word “gāḍhopagūḍhani” (very closely entwined) deserves special notice. It implies that the sinners who are afraid of approaching the Lord

[[P50]] 38’

when He stands alone, can now freely come to Him because of the constant close presence of the ‘Divine Mother’ Lakṣmī there.

The influence of the Catuśślokī on later writers was so pronounced that there arose more detailed statements about the personality and position of Lakṣmī in the form of stotras. The chief among them is the passage with which Śrī Rāmānuja’s Śaraṇāgatigadya opens. According to Rāmānuja, Śrī is the ‘Mother of the Universe’ in a general way and “of all her devotees”, in a special way.69 In this beautiful padyagandhi-piece Rāmānuja incorporates all the qualities of Lakṣmī mentioned by Yāmuna in his Catuḥślokī. Her svarūpa (essential nature), rūpa (form), guṇa (attributes), vibhava (greatness), aiśvarya (lordship) and śīla (character) are all in conformity with those of the Lord. Her qualities which are all auspicious, are innumerable and unexcelled.70 She is eternally inseparable from the Lord and she is the only person who can be safely resorted to by those who are utterly helpless (aśaraṇya-śaraṇyā).

[[P51]] 39

Rāmānuja performs Śaraṇāgati to Lakṣmī in this passage with the categorical declaration “śaraṇamahaṁ prapadye” taking the cue from the word “prapannaḥ” found in the second verse of Yāmuna’s Catuśślokī. The ŚrīSūkta has the passage ‘tāṁ padmanemīṁ śaraṇamahaṁ prapadye’ which may also be taken as the source of Rāmānuja’s statement.

The tradition of performing Śaraṇāgati to Śrī first, i.e., before doing it to the Lord, may be said to have been started by Śrī Rāmānuja. The question as to why śaraṇāgati has to be done first to Śrī may be raised here. The word ‘niravadyāṁ’ read in juxtaposition with the word ‘nityānapāyinīṁ’ which precedes it may be taken to provide the answer. Lakṣmī is always inseparably associated with Viṣṇu,which quality makes her a potent means of effecting a rapport between man and God. The word ‘niravadyāṁ’ indicates that she is ever free from ‘defects’ like anger and cruelty. This justifies Her being approachable all the time, by the devotees. Prapatti done to Lakṣmī is also explained as capable of removing all impediments and misfortunes which thwart one’s spiritual progress. The Śrīsūkta passage ‘candrāṁ prabhāsāṁ’ …. śaraṇamahaṁ prapadye’

[[P52]] 40

(ṛk.5) may be taken as indicating this particular character of Lakṣmī which has received special treatment at the hands of Rāmānuja.

Śrīvatsāṅka (alias Kūreśvara or Kūrattāḻvān) the foremost disciple of Rāmānuja, in his Śrīstava consisting of 11 verses, depicts Śrī as stimulating and guiding even Lord Viṣṇu Her consort, in His cosmic activities.71 Her motherly grace brings in alround property for all the beings.72 Basing himself on the Viṣṇupurāṇa statement wherein Lakṣmī is identified with all knowledge and learning,73 Kūreśa observes that the faculty of learning and the gift of poesy are only the result of her favour.74 She is also said to bring about activity and beauty to the entire creation by

“yajña-vidyā mahāvidyā guhyavidyā ca śobhane ātmavidyā ca devī tvam vimuktiphaladāyinī” See also Tārāpada Mandal “The worship of Lakṣmī, as Goddess of learning and wisdom”, JRAS, Vol.XXIX (No.3) 1987, p.21.

[[P53]] 41

Her mere will.75 That Lakṣmī is the source of prosperity and that Her qualities are beyond enumeration and comprehension even by Her Lord are some of the observations made by Kūreśa in the course of this poem.76 A notable feature of this stava of Kūreśa is that he identifies Śrī with Śrīraṅganāyakī the consort of Lord Raṅganātha, the presiding deity at Śrīraṅgam. What we find in Yāmuna as a description in terms of Lakṣmī’s nature and qualities without identifying her with any iconic manifestation has become an explicit reference to the arcā aspect of the Goddess in the works of his successors like Rāmānuja and Kūreśa. This idea is further reinforced in the stotras of Parāśara Bhaṭṭa and Vedānta Deśika.

The Śrīguṇaratnakośa of Parāśara explains the glory of Lakṣmī, taking into account all the earlier stotras of ācāryas. Following his father Kūreśa, Parāśara states that Lord Viṣṇu derives the impetus to create the world etc, from His

[[P54]] 42

wife Lakṣmī.77 As a matter of fact the entire cosmic activity of the Lord is nothing but a display of Her own will.78 Without Her, the Lord cannot attain His own glory.79 Being the Mother of the Universe she exhibits qualities like benevolence, forgiveness, compassion and magnanimity.80 Parāśara also, like his father, stresses that the art of poesy is the outcome of Her grace.81

Of special significance is verse 52 where Parāśara depicts Mother Śrī in the role of a Mediator. In this verse Lakṣmī is depicted as pleading with the Lord for His Mercy on behalf of the erring humanity, on the ground that there is none in the world who is free from faults.82 In another impassionate appeal Parāśara

हितस्रोतोवृत्त्या भवति च कदाचित् कल्पषधीः। किमेतत् निर्दोषः क इह जगतीति त्वमुचितैः उपायैर्विस्मार्थं स्वजनयसि माता तदसि नः।।

[[P55]] 43

seeks the forgiveness of the Divine Mother.Citing an episode from the Rāmāyaṇa, Parāśara observes that compared to Rāma, Sītā is more compassionate and benevolent. While Rāma forgave the crow and Vibhīṣaṇa only when they cried for help, Sītā saved from the wrath of Hanumān the demonesses who till a moment ago (prior to the death of Rāvaṇa) harassed her. This proves beyond doubt that even the worst sinners can seek shelter under Sītā’s grace.83

Another crucial verse which depicts Lakṣmī as the dispenser of everything that one wants including

मातर्मैथिलि राक्षसीस्त्वयि तदैवार्द्रापराधास्त्वया रक्षन्त्या पवनत्मजाल्लघुतरारोषस्य गोष्ट्ठी कृता। काकं तं च विभीषणं शरणमित्युक्ति क्षमौ रक्षतः सा नः सान्द्रमहागसः सुचरितं मानिस्त्वमेवासिका

[[P56]] 44

Mokṣa may also be cited in this context.

ऐश्वर्यमक्षरगतिं परमं पदं वा कस्मैचित् अञ्जलिभरं वहते वितायें। अस्मै न किंचित् उचितं कृतमित्यथाम्ब त्वं लज्जसे कथय कोऽयमुदारभावः Śrīguṇaratnakośa V. 58.

Even after conferring Aiśvarya (power and pelf) Kaivalya (exclusive enjoyment of the soul) and Parama-pada (the highest abode or salvation) on one who merely joins his hands in salutation, Lakṣmī feels ashamed that she could not give him anything befitting his desire. “what kind of magnanimity is this”? asks Parāśara in wonder.

It appears from this verse (50) that Goddess Lakṣmī can give all that one wants, starting from material prosperity to spiritual beatifice. It may be noted that those who argue that Lakṣmī can form the upāya of Mokṣa

[[P57]] 45

have a very strong support in this verse of Parāśara. Ultimately the author performs śaraṇāgati to Śrī and84 calls her in that spirit of resignation, the Mother, Father and all-in-all for Her devotees who take delight in rendering unconditional service to Her for ever and anon, with their friends and relative.85

Vedānta Deśika who followed these ācāryas and who became popular as the founder of the Vaḍagalai tradition, wrote the Śrīstuti in 25 verses describing the personality and position of Lakṣmī in his own unique style.86 Her vātsalya and Dayā for all the creation are again referred to. The entire creation is for the sport of the Lord and Her consort.87 Even repeating the sacred names of Mother Lakṣmī can put an end to rebirth.88 Along with those ideas Deśika also introduces a doctrinal view that the Supreme Godhead is constituted of both Viṣṇu and Lakṣmī.89

उद्दिश्यत्वं जननि भजतो रुज्झितोपाधिगन्धं प्रत्यग्रूपे हविषि युवयोरेकशेषित्वयोगात्।

[[P58]] 46

Deśika concludes his stotra with the declaration that he finds in these deities the Mother and Father of the whole creation.90

THE VAḌAGALAI AND TEṄGALAI DIFFERENCES

Although the earlier works of the ācāryas do not spell out any specific differences regarding the position of Lakṣmī, later researches into the independent treatises and commentaries written by Piḷḷai Lokācārya and Vedānta Deśika reveal some basic issues on which these two ācāryas sharply differ. Independent works like the Aṣṭādaśarahasyārthavivaraṇa91 and Aṣṭādaśabheda Nirṇaya92 have appeared highlighting the differences between the Vaḍagalai and Teṅgalai standpoints which are enumerated as eighteen in number. Of these, two points concern the nature and position of Lakṣmī.

माता देवि त्वमसि भगवान् वासुदेवः पिता मे जातः सोऽहं जननि युवयोरेक लक्ष्यं दधायाः।।

[[P59]] 47

They are: (1) the all pervasive-character of Śrī (Śrīvyāpti) and (2) regarding her upāyatva for Mokṣa or otherwise (anupāyatva).

According to the Southern School represented by Piḷḷai Lokācārya and his followers, Śrī belongs to the category of jīvas. Therefore, she is aṇu (atomic in size) like any other jīva and there is no question of her being all-pervasive like the Lord in so far as her essential nature is concerned. But there are some passages like ‘yathā sarvagato viṣṇuh tathaiveyaṁ dvijottama’ and ‘tvayaitad viṣṇunā cāmba jagadvyāptaṁ carācaram’ which seem to speak of the all-pervasive nature of Lakṣmī on par with her consort Viṣṇu. But according to this school, passageslike these refer only to what is called Vigrahavyāpti (‘corporeal pervasion”) but not Svarūpavyāpti (essential pervasion). In other words, all the female-aspect in creation is a manifestation of Lakṣmī whereas the entire male-aspect is the manifestation of Viṣṇu. It is only in this sense that Lakṣmī’s ‘pervasiveness’ can be explained, contend the followers of this tradition.

But the Northern School represented by Vedānta Deśika and his followers believe in the all-pervasive

[[P60]] 48

character of Lakṣmī as rooted in her very nature (svarūpa). In other words, Lakṣmī pervades the entire universe along with Viṣṇu so much so that both of them pervade the universe as a single indivisible principle. They form the ‘ekaśeṣin’ with reference to the entire creation which is the śeṣa. Although Lakṣmī and Nārāyaṇa are distinguishable in terms their being husband and wife functionally, they are but only one principle notionally. Their being the “Supreme Lords” of the universe may be supposed to result in dvairājya (“having two rulers”) which naturally leads to chaos and confusion. But Vedānta Deśika argues that all such doubts can be settled on the ground that though equally powerful and Supreme, one becomes subordinate to the other by their own free will.

The other point on which the Teṅgalais and Vaḍagalais clash is the upāyatva of Lakṣmī. According to the southern school, Lakṣmī by virtue of her being a jīva, although privileged to be the consort of the Lord, does not have the powers of conferring Mokṣa on the beings. This special prerogative is vested only with the Lord and nobody else. The Lord as the Brahman constitutes the sole cause of the creation, sustenance and dissolution of the universe. This

[[P61]] 49

is what the Upaniṣad declares93 and the Brahmasūtra confirms.94 By the same token, Brahman alone can be the source of liberation of the Universe, and no other being, however powerful it may be, can discharge this function. But contrary to this, Vedānta Deśika argues that Lakṣmī can constitute the upāya of Mokṣa as well. A number of scriptural passages and Āgama declarations are in support of this view, he argues. Eg. the following passage from the Brahmapurāṇa: -

सर्वकामप्रदा रम्या संसारार्णवतारिणीम्। क्षिप्रप्रसादिनीं लक्ष्मीं धारण्यामनुचिन्तयेत्।।

and the Viṣṇupurāṇa-passage (I.9.20)

आत्मविद्या च देवि त्वं विमुक्ति फलदायिनी।।

clearly define Lakṣmī’s role as the bestower of Vimukti.

Thus broadly speaking, the vibhutva and upāyatva of Lakṣmī form the two main issues on which these two

यतो वा इमानि भूतानि जायन्ते येन जातानि जीवन्ति यत्प्रयन्त्यभिसंविशन्ति

[[P62]] 50

schools are divided. All other points concerning Lakṣmī are only secondary in nature. The points on which these two schools agree are (1) that Śrī is the consort of the Lord, (2) that she is the mother of the universe and (3) that she is capable and willing to be the puruṣakāra between man and God. They also agree on one more point namely, that she also forms the upeya- the ultimate goal of human endeavour, in receiving kaiṅkarya rendered by the jīva-s in the company of the Lord.

The ideas on the status of Lakṣmī are to be found elaborately discussed and interpreted in the commentorial literature that sprang up during the 12th and 13th centuries. The Śrīsūkta commented upon by Raṅganātha and the commentaries on the Catuśślokī by Periyavāccān Piḷḷai (PV), his son Nainārāccān Piḷḷai and Vedānta Deśika, and the Śrīvacana Bhūṣaṇa, Mumukṣuppaḍi and other texts of Piḷḷai Lokācārya, and the commentaries of Maṇavāḷa Mahāmuṇi, yield rich information regarding Lakṣmī’s status. It is really surprising to see that a simple and straightforward hymn of Yāmuna became the pivot of all these interpretational differences in later times.

[[P63]] 51

Raṅganātha95 in his commentary on the Śrīsūkta, especially on the 9th Ṛk “Īśvarīṁ sarvabhūtānām” enters into a detailed discussion on the concept of Śrī. He identifies the Śruti epithets like śraddhā and ‘Viṣṇupatnī’ with Śrī. Śrī and Viṣṇu on the authority of the Ahirbudhnya Saṁhitā and Lakṣmī Tantra are said to be engaged in saving the souls, which activity is likened to a sacrifice.96 He argues that both the Lord and His consort are the masters and saviours of this world. This position does not contradict Brahman’s being advitīya. Since Śrī and Viṣṇu form a couple, such a consequence is ruled out, he contends. Some texts speaks of the Lord alone as the sovereign of the world while some others speak of Lakṣmī as the sovereign. Since both these passages are equally valid, we have to conclude that the universe has but a single supreme principle in the form of the couple Lakṣmī-Nārāyaṇa, concludes Raṅganātha. Quoting the well known passage -

अकारोच्यते विष्णुः सर्वलोकेश्वरो हरिः। उद्भूता विष्णुना लक्ष्मीरुकारोच्यते तथा।। मकारस्तु तयोर्दास इति प्रणवलक्षणम्।।

[[P64]] 52

and attributing it to one Kathāśruti,97 Raṅganātha remarks that Bhagavān, the import of the sacred syllable “अ” is the ruler of the entire universe. This is the connotation of the first sentence quoted above. That Lakṣmī is the import of syllable “उ” is clear from the second sentence. The additional point which Raṅganātha gives here is that the word ’tathā’ found in the second line which means “in a similar manner”, becomes significant only when Lakṣmī also is understood as ‘Sarvalokeśvarī’. Since the jīva (‘makāra’) is called the servant of both, Raṅganātha remarks that Lakṣmī and Nārāyaṇa constitute the śeṣin severally with reference to the individual souls. But this does not impair their being one unitary principle.उभयशिष्टे चेश्वरत्वात् explain the śeṣitva or Lordship of Lakṣmī and Nārāyaṇa with reference to the jīvas does not present them as two different masters each having a different control over them, but that both of them form a single two-in-one couple ruling over the entire creation. Raṅganātha corroborates his observation with quotations from the Ahirbudhnya Saṁhitā98 and the Sāgārtha Saṁkṣepa99 of

[[P65]] 53

Śrīrāmaṁiśra.’ He also observes that though Lakṣmī and Viṣṇu are independent of each other regarding their status, one is still subordinate to the other. This is only voluntary but not obligatory.100

Periyavāccān Piḷḷai (PP), the illustrious son of Vaṭakkuttiruvīthi Piḷḷai in his maṇipravāḷa commentary on Yāmuna’s Catuśślokī makes some important observations regarding the nature and status of Lakṣmī. According to him, the opening verse of the Catuśślokī establishes the paratva of Lakṣmī in association with Viṣṇu. The second verse brings out her role as Puruṣakāra. The third one presents Lakṣmī as an integral component of jagatkāraṇa and that she is the upāya of liberation. The fourth verse according to him, describes the nature of the ultimate result or phala, namely, that in all His manifestations the Lord is also associated with Lakṣmī who also takes suitable manifestations, which forms part of their cosmic Līlā or divine sport. All these four verses point to Lord Nārāyaṇa who is qualified by Lakṣmī as the ultimate goal of all living beings.101

[[P66]] 54

Periyavāccān Piḷḷai is of the view that the highest goal of all living beings (paramapuruṣārtha) is to be subordinate to the Divine couple Lakṣmī-Nārāyaṇa.102 He quotes the Kaṭhaśruti (‘akārepocyate etc’) to justify this point. Commenting on the word ‘śrīḥ’ he observes that Lakṣmī is called Śrī because she forms the mistress of all living beings (Sarvaśeṣiṇī). He derives this word from the root ‘śriñ-sevāyām’ (śriñ means ’to serve’, ’to resort to’) (to be understood in the passive sense).

The second verse addresses Lakṣmī as ’lokaikeśvarī’ which, according to Periyavāccān Piḷḷai is yet another clear description of Lakṣmī’s position as śeṣiṇī (mistress with reference to the entire universe). As to the question how Lakṣmī could become the śeṣiṇī of the universe, Periyavāccān Piḷḷai answers it with the epithet ’lokanāthadayitā’. Since she is the consort of the master of the universe, she automatically becomes the mistress of the universe. Although she is the śeṣiṇī with reference to the world, She is śeṣa with reference to the Lord who is

[[P67]] 55

para (‘Supreme’). Still, there is no contradiction because, we do not have two śeṣins with reference to the universe.103

Although he mentions that the second verse of the Catuśślokī brings out Lakṣmī’s role as Puruṣakāra,104 he does not elaborate this idea anywhere in his commentary. On the whole it is clear that Periyavāccān Piḷḷai seems to favour the following concepts regarding Lakṣmī

a) That she is Puruṣakāra. b) That she also is the jagatkāraṇa. c) That she is the upāya. d) That she qualifies the Lord as an adjective and that the ultimate goal is a single God-head, namely Lakṣmī-Nārāyaṇa. e) Though she is also the śeṣiṇī with reference to the universe along with the Lord, she still forms a śeṣa to Him so much so that there is only one śeṣi-principle and there is no duality in it.

[[P68]] 56

f) The only purpose of human life is to be subordinate to such a divine couple.

It may be noted that Periyavāccān Piḷḷai was a senior contemporary of Vedānta Deśika and these ideas of his might have influenced Deśika also.

The Catuśślokī received another commentary from Nainārāccān Piḷḷai (NP), son of Periyavāccān Piḷḷai. It is significant to note that Nainārāccān Piḷḷai’s views on the nature and status of Lakṣmī take a different route altogether, giving perhaps his own personal conclusions, not influenced by his father’s thoughts on the subject.

His views correspond to those of Piḷḷai Lokācāriar who represents the Teṅgalai sect. It may be also noted that Vedānta Deśika quotes some of his views and refutes them in his commentary on the Catuśślokī. Some of the important discussions found in NP’s commentary on verse 3 of the Catuśślokī (NPC) may be summarised as follows:

NP takes up the question of mokṣapradatva (the capacity to confer Mokṣa) and after a detailed discussion concludes that Īśvara alone bestows Mokṣa and that too, after Lakṣmī’s mediation.105 Regarding the statement which

[[P69]] 57

speaks of the Lord protecting the universe in association with Lakṣmī, (‘Lakṣmyā saha hṛṣīkeśaḥ’ etc) he observes that the employment of the indeclinable ‘saha’ along with the Instrumental Case indicates the secondary status of Lakṣmī (‘apradhāne tṛtīyā’). In other words, she only becomes Puruṣakāra and not upāya. He also argues that even the Dvaya-mantra indicates her status as Puruṣakāra, which is quite unique for Her.106 Lakṣmī, like other attributes, becomes a Viśeṣaṇa of the Lord. That being the case, how can guṇas alone be accepted as upāya, and Lakṣmī as Puruṣakāra alone? Anticipating this question NP answers that attributes, do bear an inherent relationship with a specific activity (kārya), connected with the person whom they qualify. But Lakṣmī, as distinguished from the epithets of the Lord, has no direct involvement in the activity (Kārya) of the Lord such as creation., etc. That is the reason why Lakṣmī is treated as Puruṣakāra, and guṇas as upāya.107

If Lakṣmī is also said to confer Mokṣa then, certain qualities of the Lord such as jñāna and śakti (omniscience and omnipotence) which alone make the Lord the conferer of Mokṣa will become redundant and insignificant. If,

[[P70]] 58

however, we accept these qualities in the Lord, then Lakṣmī will have to be set aside because her presence becomes redundant.108 Therefore, the best way out of this controversy is to accept Lakṣmī as having mere “puruṣakāratva” and that she has nothing to do with upāyatva. It may then be asked as to how the passages describing Lakṣmī as the bestower of Mokṣa, ‘vimukti phaladāyinī’109 can be understood, in the event of her not being the actual bestower of Mokṣa.

NP answers that these passages merely indicate that Lakṣmī acts in conformity with the opinion or will of the “Śaraṇya” namely the Lord and that they never stress her actual authority in conferring Mokṣa.110 He argues that the nature of Lakṣmī has been decided once for all in the Pāñcarātrāgamas as being the “Puruṣakāra” alone and as having no other status. For example, the following texts categorically declare this point:

[[P71]] 59

लक्ष्मी पुरुषकारेण वृतवन्तः वशनम् (Viṣvaksena Saṁhitā)

“लक्ष्मीः पुरुषकारेण वक्ष्मा प्राश्योजिनी। प्रत्यक्षश्रा विदितोऽयं निगमान्तेषु शब्द्यते।। लक्ष्मीः पुरुषकारत्वे निर्दिष्टा परमर्षिभिः। मयापि च मतं ह्येतन्मान्यथा तक्षणं भवेत्।।”

NP concludes this discussion with the observation that the chief characteristic of Lakṣmī is “Puruṣakāratva” and nothing else.111

The next question raised by NP is whether Lakṣmī has all-pervasiveness by her essential nature or not. It appears on the prima facie view that unless she is vibhu by nature, she cannot be the consort of Lord Nārāyaṇa, who by His very nature is vibhu (all-pervasive). Answering in the negative NP says that of the Three Realities accepted in the system, Lakṣmī does not form part of insentient matter for the very reason that she is a conscious being. Nor does she become part of the

[[P72]] 60

Īśvara-category because the scriptures declare Īśvara to be one without a second (cf. Śve. Up.I.10 ईक्षते देव एकः). Therefore the only alternative is that she belongs to the category of jīva. Since jīva is aṇu, she must also be an aṇu.112

It may be contended that though being a jīva she may still be taken as vibhu but not aṇu. But this argument is preposterous because then the definition of jīva namely, “aṇutve sati cetanatvam”, becomes avyāpta (non-pervasive) in her case. Further, the definition of Īśvara, namely, ‘vibhutve sati cetanutvam’ becomes over-pervasive in the case of Lakṣmī. Therefore, it is but reasonable to accept Lakṣmī as a jīva characterised by aṇutva which is quite natural to her.

As for the statements that she is as pervasive as the Lord,113 he answers that they are to be interpreted as speaking of the corporeal pervasion (vigraha-vyāpti) but

[[P73]] 61

not essential pervasion (svarūpa-vyāpti) of Lakṣmī. Therefore it is to be concluded that Lakṣmī is aṇu and not vibhu like the Lord. NP tries to prove this point with the help of syllogistic statements also.114

Another prima facie view raised is that although Lakṣmī is aṇu she may still assume vibhutva because of her wonderful powers.115 In reply NP says that if such an allowance is given to Lakṣmī, then even other systems of thought like Advaita and Bhedābheda may also become acceptable and valid because oneness of everything and difference-cum-identity can be accounted for by the mysterious powers of Brahman.116

In the light of the views discussed so far, it is but proper now to assess the views of Piḷḷai Lokācārya the senior contemporary of Vedānta Deśika on the nature and position of Lakṣmī. His works like the Śrīvacana Bhūṣaṇa

[[P74]] 62

(ŚVB) and the Mumukṣuppaḍi speak out his standpoint in clear terms. In the opening section of the ŚVB he points out that the Rāmāyaṇa which speaks of the greatness of Sītā brings out her glory as Puruṣakāra. The Mahābhārata speaks of the greatness of the Lord who is the upāya i.e., means of protecting the living beings.117

Commenting on this passage Maṇavāḷa Mahāmuni (MM) quotes passages from the Pāñcarātrāgamas referred to earlier and concludes that the unique characteristic of Lakṣmī is ‘puruṣakāratva’ alone and the very purpose of PL, writing this section is to emphasize this point.118

According to PL, ‘puruṣakāratva’ involves three qualities: Kṛpā (mercy) which consists in one’s not being able to bear with the misery of others; pāratantrya (dependence on the person whom the aggrieved wants to approach for help) and ananyārhatva (being totally in tune with that particular person whose grace is sought, and not with anybody else). To explain, Lakṣmī cannot bear with

[[P75]] 63

the sufferings of beings caught up in transmigration and tries to bring them to the Lord. This naturally anticipates the quality of mercy on her part. The Lord being independent, she has to be in conformity with his will because only then she will be able to convince him about the deserving nature of the jīvas. This requires Lakṣmī to be subordinate to the Lord. Thirdly, the Lord also must feel that what she is doing is, in the long run, going to result in His own excellence. This is what is meant by ananyārhatva. That Lakṣmī alone satisfies these three conditions, that there is no need for the jīvas to seek another Puruṣakāra, that Lakṣmī is pāratantra and that she deserves Him alone and nobody else (ananyārha) are the features natural to Her. This is corroborated by a number of Śruti-passages.119

PL argues that these three qualities which make Lakṣmī (Sītā) the Puruṣakāra are illustrated by the three occasions when Sītā got separated from Rāma. The first occasion when Sītā was separated from Rāma was when Rāvaṇa took her away and made her a captive in Laṅkā.

[[P76]] 64

Then Sītā brought out her quality of Kṛpā. The reference is to her saving the wives of gods who were imprisoned by Rāvaṇa in Laṅkā. Only because she was kept captive in Laṅkā Rāma had to go there and set her free, which also resulted in the liberation of all those goddesses. The commentator remarks that Sītā’s saving the demonesses from the wrath of Hanumān who wanted to kill them, may also be cited as an illustration of her Kṛpā.

Sītā’s separation from Her Lord for the second time took place when he abandoned her in an advanced stage of pregnancy. This, according to PL, is to demonstrate her quality of Pāratantrya (total dependence on her Lord). She acted according to the will of the Lord and faced the consequences without a question. This demonstrates that Sītā is always pāratantra but not svatantrā.

The third separation according to PL is the voluntary separation which Sītā undertook towards the end of the Epic, when she prayed for her Mother Earth to open up and take her with her. The purpose of this separation is to show that she deserves the Lord alone and nobody else. Apprehending that Rāma would be asking for another proof

[[P77]] 65

for her chastity, although he himself was convinced about it and although the words of Vālmīki himself could have cleared the last doubt in his mind, Sītā, dressed in kāṣāya, stood before all and prayed with folded hands to Her mother Earth, to take her with her, swearing on her virtue namely, ‘ananyārhatva’ ’that she exists for Rāma alone and for nobody else’.120

PL continues that the Puruṣakāratva of Lakṣmī continues to show itself when she is in association with the Lord and also when she was away from Him. Episodes are cited from the Rāmāyaṇa to illustrate this point.121

She corrects the Lord when she is associated with Him and when she is separated from Him, she corrects the erring souls. In any case she does it through her timely advice. It is this advice that makes the Lord give up his stand that the jīvas suffer as a result of their own past Karma, and saves them. With regard to the jīvas also her timely advice

[[P78]] 66

punctuated by her motherly love, makes them free from their own Karma which is the result of their beginning-less avidyā. She becomes acceptable to both the Lord and jīvas by virtue of her being the beloved consort and benign mother respectively. PL further observes that when her advice does not have the desired effect she goes a step further - she corrects the Lord by winning Him by her beautiful form and she puts the jīvas in the right by her overwhelming compassion.122

In his other works also which are popularly known as Rahasyas PL brings out the Puruṣakāratva of Lakṣmī on several occasions. In his Śriyappatipadi, in the section on Dvaya he observes that as Puruṣakāra between man and God she has close relationships with both – as the mother with jīvas and as the consort with the Lord. Explaining the word “Śrī” etymologically as Śṛṇoti (one who listens) and Śrāvayati (one who makes the Lord listen), PL argues that Lakṣmī listens to the piteous appeals of the erring living beings and makes the Lord

[[P79]] 67

also listen to them, shed his anger, and take pity upon the jīvas. This is in keeping with her nature as Puruṣakāra. He also observes that one has to do śaraṇāgati to the Lord only in the presence of Lakṣmī; otherwise the desired effect of śaraṇāgati may not be attained in full.123

In his Parandapaḍi, section on Dvaya, explaining the stem “Śrīmat”, PL points out that both the Puruṣakāra and nityayoga of Lakṣmī are implied here. The term ‘Śrī’ is explained through two etymologies “Śrīyate” (she who is resorted to) and ‘śrayate’ (she who resorts to). The qualities which are expected of a Puruṣakāra are very well brought out in this work. One important quality is ubhaya-sambandha, that she is related to both the beings and God. She is related to the beings as their ‘Mother’ and to the Lord as His consort. Because she is the Mother of all the beings, she is all the time approachable by them, even without any recourse to her role as Puruṣakāra. Being related to the Lord as His eternal and inseparable consort she always serves Him spontaneously without any recourse to her Puruṣakāratva. The other interpretations of the term Śrī are Śṛṇoti (she listens to) and Śrāvayati (she makes the Lord listen to). The jīva, because of

[[P80]] 68

his crimes perpetuated from time immemorial, and being apprehensive of the Lord’s anger who says, “I will throw them into hell” ( क्षिपामि )124 and ‘I will not tolerate ( न क्षमामि )’125 will naturally turn to the Mother for necessary help. The Goddess listens to their appeals and passes them on to the Lord also for favour of his consideration.126

In his Tanidvayam, ‘Pūrvavākya’, PL points out that Śrī is not similar either to the jīvas or to the Lord. Explaining that the term ‘Śrī’ brings out her specific role as ‘Puruṣakāra’ through the two etymologies ‘Śṛṇoti’ and ‘Śrāvayati’ as interpreted earlier, PL declares that the essential nature of the Lord is one of unlimited pervasion, whereas the essential nature of Lakṣmī which is very subtle like an aṇu (atom) becomes ‘great’ as the ‘bhogya’ (object of enjoyment) of the Lord. Lakṣmī combines in herself two qualities – vaṁinitva - with reference to the world of Cit and Acit, and also patnītva with reference to the Lord, both being sanctioned by pramāṇas. There is a passage “puṁspradhāneśvareśvarī” (Lakṣmī Sahasranāma) where she is said to be the ruler of not only the jīvas

[[P81]] 69

and Material Nature but also of the Lord. PL states out that this passage points to the total dependence of the Lord on the love of Lakṣmī because she is so attractive. Therefore there is no contradiction in the statements that although she is aṇu in nature, her glory is ‘all-pervasive’ like the Lord’s. PL brings in the analogy of two flowers red in colour of which one is bereft of fragrance and the other is of natural fragrance. The glory of Lakṣmī is comparable to the latter, points out PL.127 ‘If Lakṣmī is said to be superior to the Lord by virtue of her inherent qualities, what else is there to be discussed?’ he asks.

The universe being subordinate to both Viṣṇu and Lakṣmī, she becomes an object of worship to the universe. She also becomes adorable to the Lord because of Her lovable character which is inherent in Her very nature. One and the same lady may be the mistress to her children and at the same time subordinate to her husband. Her husband in turn will also be subordinate to her because

[[P82]] 70

of Her personal charm. This does not in any way affect the independence of the Lord. The concept of Puruṣakāra is also a matter of day to day experience says PL. Because of her affable character a woman becomes the protector of her children. The father, a strict disciplinarian, may sometimes behave harsh towards his children, of course in their own interest. But still they seek shelter under their Mother and she argues with her husband that the offences committed by them are very minor and that they do not deserve any serious punishment. The Puruṣakāratva of Lakṣmī is similar to this, concludes PL. She always abides by the Lord with the only view that she can always function as Puruṣakāra between Him and the creatures. The Lord is sarvajña (omnicient) and the jīvas are sāparādha (full of offence). Therefore the presence of Puruṣakāra between them is required for eternity.

Thus from the works of PL we may conclude that according to him (a) Lakṣmī is a jīva and hence aṇu (atomic) in size. This is her svarūpa; (b) She is always associated with the Lord as His consort; (c) She is always the kind and compassionate mother of all the beings. She therefore is superbly suited to act as the Puruṣakāra between them; (d) the Lord Himself, though vibhu by his

[[P83]] 71

essential nature, is always subordinate to her, being charmed by her love and affection. She forms the śeṣiṇī for the entire universe and thus becomes its object of adoration. She also becomes the chief object of love to her Lord because of her exquisite charm and wonderful love.

Meghanādāri Sūri (MS) probably a senior contemporary of Vedānta Deśika, in his work Nayadyumaṇi (ND) discusses the position of Lakṣmī.128 He is of the view that Lakṣmī is not mentioned in the Upaniṣads as the cause of the universe. Therefore she has to be included among the Nityamuktas. Nowhere the Vedāntas speak of two causes of the Universe. In the Vedārthasaṁgraha, Rāmānuja refers to Lord Nārāyaṇa alone as the jagatkāraṇa and establishes that he has consorts, entourage and the like. Otherwise, if the mere presence of Lakṣmī alone constitutes the causality, he would have mentioned the couple as jagatkāraṇa. But this is not so. The Upaniṣads too have not stated that a couple constitutes the cause. On the other hand, texts like the Puruṣa Sūkta

[[P84]] 72

speak of the creation as emanating from one Puruṣa as a result of his saṅkalpa. The passage ‘adbhyaḥ saṁbhūtaḥ’129 refers to this and the succeeding passage “hrīśca te lakṣmīśca patnyau”130 ascribes to Him the consortship of Lakṣmī and Hrī. That is to say, He has three wives but not one since we already know Earth as the other consort of Viṣṇu through passages like ‘mahīṁ devīṁ viṣṇupatnīm’.131 The word “hrī” of the Puruṣa Sūkta text must refer to another wife, namely Nīḷā. Śrī Rāmānuja also says ’evambhūtā-bhūminīḷānāyaka’ etc.132

Inspite of this declaration one cannot fix a single couple as the cause of the universe. The word ‘couple’ refers to one husband and one wife. Since there are three wives here, we cannot refer to all of them as forming the couple with the Lord. Nor is any one of these three consorts specifically referred to in any authoritative text as forming the couple with the Lord for purposes of

अद्भ्यःसंभूतो हिरण्यगर्भ इत्यष्टौ

[[P85]] 73

creation, maintenance and dissolution of the universe. On the contrary, from passages like ‘viṣṇoḥ sakāśāt udbhūtam’ (VP. 1-1-31) it is clear that Viṣṇu alone, to the exclusion of any of His consorts, forms the cause of the Universe.133

Meghanādāri Sūri then takes up the statements found in some of the stotras which attribute causality to the divine couple and discusses their little credibility. For example, there is a statement ’tvaṁ mātā sarvalokānāṁ devadevo Hariḥ pitā’, where a couple seems to be the cause. The author answers in the negative, stating that in the given context Śrī alone forms the subject of praise as the Mother of the entire universe and that as such, no importance need be attached to the couple as the jagatkāraṇa. As for the passage quoted earlier, viz., ‘Viṣṇoḥ sakāśāt’ this is found clearly in the context of cosmic causality, and hence it has to be accepted as a pramāṇa. Therefore if some of the stotras, seem to declare any divine person other than the Lord Viṣṇu (including the couple Lakṣmī-Nārāyaṇa) as the cause of the universe, they have to be taken

[[P86]] 74

as eulogising that particular person for the purposes of the stotra, but not as a serious statement regarding the causality of the world. For example, Balarāma who is an incarnation of Ādiśeṣa is praised as the jagatkāraṇa as the one in whom everything merges in the end:134

’tadarcyate vetsi na kiṁ yadante tvayyeva viśvam’

Ādiśeṣa whose aspect Balarāma is, is one of the Ever-liberated souls, (nityamukta) who have no power with regard to cosmic activities.135 Therefore if the stotras are at variance with established pramāṇas we cannot accord primary significance to them.

It may be asked that if Lakṣmī is not the cause, how can she be referred to as the Mother. Meghanādāri answers that because she is the consort of the Supreme Brahman, she can be called the Mother of the universe. As a matter of fact, in day to day life too, the word ‘mother’ is very loosely used even with reference to the other wives of one’s father, even though none of

[[P87]] 75

them is actually the source of one’s birth. The real and the direct Mother is the Lord alone and none else,136 contends Meghanādāri.

He is therefore in favour of Lakṣmī’s being one among the ever-liberated souls and as such he implies that she is only aṇu in parimāṇa.137

Vātsya Varadācārya (VV), a senior contemporary of Vedānta Deśika, in his Prapannapārijāta (PP), in Paddhati VI, tries to show that Lakṣmī is vibhu like Lord Viṣṇu. He relies upon the Viśvaksena Saṁhitā.138 for this purpose. This Āgama declares that the entire universe is pervaded by the Lord and Lakṣmī and in this aspect there is no difference at all between them.

[[P88]] 76

Vyāpti (pervasion) can be from two standpoints: guṇas (qualities) and svarūpa (essential nature). Both kinds of Vyāpti are there in the case of Lakṣmī, as in the case of the Lord. She is pervaded by the Lord and the Lord is pervaded by Her. But the difference between them consists in this: although she is the ruler of all the beings, She is subordinate to Him and is His beloved too. The Lord is the Supreme ruler with reference to Lakṣmī as well as the universe. The entire universe consisting of both the līlā and nitya vibhūtis is subordinate to the Lord and Lakṣmī. As for His two other consorts Bhūmi and Nīlā, they are subordinate to the Lord.
